Test results showed that the system was only capable of releasing up to 6 lbs of static loading and less than 3 lbs of loading during flights.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c11">The second payload drop system had been designed to tackle all of the issues that arose in the first iteration, and was entirely manufactured out of 3D printed ABS. The distance between the servo and the brackets was reduced to a distance of 4.53 </span><span class="c0">in</span><span class="c10">. The 1:1 ratio between the bracket thickness and distance between the two brackets was restored, and the attachment point between the pushrod and servo arm was moved from the third hole to the second hole. While the movement of the attachment point requires a larger sweep angle from the servo, it allows the pushrod to travel a more linear path than the previous design allowed, thus reducing pinching and making the tradeoff worthwhile. The new bracket was also designed to fit aluminum spacers as guides for the pushrod. This enabled a lower coefficient of friction compared to stainless steel on ABS. The spacers would also allow the pushrod to move in and out of the bracket without the worry of losing material as seen in the acrylic pieces.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">The final drop system design proved to be a successful release mechanism for at least 11.5 lbs of static loading with a 100% success rate during bench level testing, and was able to release the 5 lb payload during the final flight test on May 1, 2019.</span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><a id="t.a742cf8293f3765210c215d722dffc6057b4415f"></a><a id="t.8"></a><table class="c65"><tbody><tr class="c72"><td class="c104" colspan="2" rowspan="1"><p class="c30"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 300.00px; height: 152.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image76.png" style="width: 300.00px; height: 152.50px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 299.50px; height: 152.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image78.png" style="width: 299.50px; height: 152.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c6 c23"><span class="c10">Figure 14. Final design iteration of the servo-pushrod release system.</span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><h3 class="c40 c23" id="h.60t5zftm20xx"><span class="c28 c21">2.4.3 Paracord Drop System [AH]</span></h3><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c11">The paracord drop system consists of an Emax </span><span class="c11">ES08maII</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;servo, 40 m of type IV paracord, and a velcro strap to hold the paracord snug to the MTD. One end of the strap is fixed to the MTD frame, while the other end is held up by the servo. The Emax </span><span class="c11">ES08maII</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;servo is chosen because of its previous success of similar releases from the competition UAV. Type IV paracord is selected for its durability and functional application as a retrieval cord. The velcro strap is selected for its light weight and adjustability for different fittings. </span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">&nbsp;When the servo is activated, the unfixed end of the velcro strap is released and the paracord is dropped, with one end of the paracord remaining tethered to the payload compartment. Multiple tests have proven that the figure-eight coil prevents tangling and the servo-strap system successfully releases the paracord with a 100% success rate. </span></p><p class="c15 c22"><span class="c10">During an initial flight test, the MTD was unable to drag the paracord through grass, so a paracord tension test was performed to see how much tension force is applied to the MTD via the paracord. The 40 m of paracord was attached to a fishing scale and was dragged through the grass at varying speeds, producing an average of 0.23 Ns/m. Thus, the MTD cruising speed of approximately 16 m/s provides a small tension force of 3.7 N, showing that the CG and stability of the UAV are critical. Recent flight tests have proven that the UAV is able to drag majority of the paracord through the grass and have a successful subsequent altitude climb. </span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><h3 class="c40 c23" id="h.nhv5nd7nwd5"><span>2.4.4 &nbsp;Stability with Full Delivery System [AH, SO]</span></h3><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">Moment calculations were performed to ensure stable flight with the full weight of the delivery system attached to the UAV using equation 3 (see Fig X for corresponding free body diagram). </span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><a id="t.e220f3678d346b61c771f53ae2c94205afd0f6e0"></a><a id="t.9"></a><table class="c65"><tbody><tr class="c8"><td class="c126" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c23"><img src="images/image9.png"></p></td><td class="c111" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">(3)</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">where W is the weight of the UAV and delivery system, F</span><span class="c11 c67">py</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;and F</span><span class="c11 c67">px</span><span class="c11">&nbsp; are the paracord forces, and </span><span class="c11">D</span><span class="c11 c67">payload</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;and </span><span class="c11">D</span><span class="c11 c67">plane</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;are the drag forces. The velocity of the UAV was set to the cruise speed of 16 m/s, and the corresponding forces at the cruise speed are F</span><span class="c11 c67">py</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;=</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;0.8669 N, F</span><span class="c11 c67">px</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;= 3.597 N, D</span><span class="c11 c67">plane</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;= 10.1338 N, and D</span><span class="c11 c67">payload</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;= 0.8438 N.</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 330.74px; height: 248.96px;"><img alt="" src="images/image34.png" style="width: 330.74px; height: 248.96px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span class="c10">Figure 15. Free body diagram for moment calculations about the AC location.</span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">To avoid needing the maximum thrust, the location of the CG was manipulated while the targeted thrust values required for stable flight were kept near 45 N. Table 8 shows the resulting CG placements at various increments of weight. As a general rule of thumb, the CG location should be moved approximately 3 mm closer to the leading edge of the wing as the weight is increased by 1.25 lbs. Note that these calculations are for stable flight once the paracord is already released. Thus, the starting CG is placed slightly farther back than the desired CG location that is shown in Table 8. For example, the starting CG for the successful 5 lb flight on May 1st was approximately 10.1 cm from the leading edge of the wing, as opposed to the desired 9.9 cm CG location derived from the moment calculations. &nbsp;</span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span class="c10">Table 8. CG placements and required thrusts for added weights with the payload compartment. *[cm] from the leading edge of the wing.</span></p><a id="t.4a163d941d84c229f74001b7f5bef4ee2c2661d2"></a><a id="t.10"></a><table class="c73"><tbody><tr class="c8"><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Weight Added [lbs]</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">CG Placement* [cm]</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c11">F</span><span class="c11 c67">T</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;[N]</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">1.25</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">10.8</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">45.0434</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">2.50</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">10.5</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">46.3346</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">3.75</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">10.2</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">46.434</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">5.00</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">9.9</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">45.3408</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">6.25</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">9.6</span></p></td><td class="c3" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">43.0569</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">During the April 17th flight test, stability of the payload compartment had been questioned on whether or not swaying of the payload compartment had been the source of oscillations. Oscillations could occur if there were any gaps between the bracket and payload attachment piece. </span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">In response, two strips of high density packing foam were placed between the payload compartment and the fuselage of the UAV to act as a damper. The foam was secured to the payload compartment via the hose clamp used for the payload attachment piece.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">During the April 21st flight test, the payload compartment failed to release while in air. An emergency landing had been performed. It was noted that the foam could have been putting too much force against the fuselage and payload compartment creating additional external forces on the pushrod causing the release mechanism to fail. </span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">New stabilizers were then fabricated out of spare attachment pieces. The reasoning for the modifying the spare attachment pieces came from the idea that it already had a way to be secured to the payload compartment and it did not necessary have to put any additional forces onto the pushrod because it could be adjusted to fit anywhere along the hose clamp. The stabilizers modified out of spare attachment pieces are shown in Figure 16.</span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 244.00px; height: 244.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image52.jpg" style="width: 244.00px; height: 244.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 243.00px; height: 243.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image41.jpg" style="width: 243.00px; height: 243.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span class="c10">Figure 16. Front and side view of stabilizers fabricated from spare payload attachment pieces.</span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">Two of these stabilizers were placed on either end of the payload attachment piece on the hose clamp. The stabilizers would be set into place after the payload attachment piece was secured in by the pushrod. </span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">During the in lab testing, it was seen that the delivery system had been able to drop 7 lbs consistently if and only if the pushrod a correct path through the second end of the bracket.It had been noted that the pushrod sometimes failed to path correctly, and insteads slips under the second end of the bracket causing more &ldquo;pinching&rdquo; than allowed. Against the concerns, a flight test was conducted on April 23rd with the notion that the pushrod path would be verifiable.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">During the April 23rd flight test, the UAV failed to release the payload compartment and it was noted that the cause could have been from two different sources: the stabilizers or the pushrod itself. Because the stabilizers had been set after the payload attachment piece had been secured, the stabilizers could have been creating additional force on the pushrod just as the foam had done. One of the main discrepancies came from having to adjust the stabilizers and hose clamp after the payload compartment was secured to the pushrod, which meant that no trial could have the exact same fit even with markings along the hose clamp. It was noted that the stabilizers did not have much room for adjustment between the fuselage and payload compartment because of its fabricated dimensions.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">New stabilizers were designed to allow a wider range of adjustments between the fuselage and payload compartment. The new stabilizers were fabricated out of ABS via 3D printing and can be seen in Figure 17.</span></p><p class="c6 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 213.00px; height: 194.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image18.jpg" style="width: 213.00px; height: 213.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: -19.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 264.50px; height: 192.20px;"><img alt="" src="images/image28.jpg" style="width: 264.50px; height: 265.34px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span class="c10">Figure 17. Front and side view of 3D printed stabilizers (left). Stabilizer orientation on hose clamp (right).</span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">To address the pushrod alignment issue, a new servo baseboard had been designed and 3D printed as mentioned in a previous section. Because of the design of the 3D printed baseboard and its removal of errors resulting from hand fabrication, the payload compartment fit snug against the fuselage with no play for oscillations. The successful May 1st flight test had verified that stabilizers were no longer needed to be considered.</span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">The 3D printed servo baseboard had also been designed to include fasteners for the PixHawk, which not only helped the stability of the MTD, but also increased repairability. To ensure that the PixHawk would integrate smoothly, a PixHawk bracket was designed and 3D printed out of ABS as shown in Figure 18.</span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 196.50px; height: 196.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image30.jpg" style="width: 196.50px; height: 196.50px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 198.50px; height: 198.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image15.jpg" style="width: 198.50px; height: 198.50px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 199.50px; height: 199.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image31.jpg" style="width: 199.50px; height: 199.50px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span class="c10">Figure 18: Isotropic view of PixHawk and bracket (left); bottom view (middle). Integration on base (right). </span></p><p class="c6 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><h1 class="c40 c23" id="h.m7hohwxb77n3"><span>3.0</span><span>&nbsp;Testing and Evaluation Results</span></h1><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c21 c138"></span></p><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.i5nlos2ua81e"><span>3</span><span>.1 Aircraft </span></h2><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">3.1.1 Payload Capacity [MS, CR]</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">The final flight test on May 1st validated our aircrafts requirements of being able to generate more than a sufficient amount of lift while holding a 2.3 kg payload. The weight of the payload was measured to be 2.31 kg and the wind condition at the time of take off was in the range of 5-7 knots. On this flight test &nbsp;the throttle percent was set to 80 percent and the thrust was at 57 N, with this configuration the MTD was able to takeoff with ease. </span></p><p class="c9 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">3.1.2 Wind Conditions [MS]</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">Through multiple flight tests at Bellows Air Force Base, the average wind conditions were measured to be between 7 and 10 knots with gusts of up to 15 knots. Although the sudden gusts did cause some spikes in altitude the flights in those wind conditions were stable as long as our CG placement was within our margin of stability. The requirement of operating in 10 knot winds was met and validated on these successful flight tests. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.cyd68fmpwq2t"><span>3.2</span><span>&nbsp;</span><span>Electronics</span></h2><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.o7mrqi9cjxqx"><span class="c28 c21">3.2.1 Battery Life [KT]</span></h3><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c11">Looking at 5 different consistent and successful flight tests where the battery drop was noted, the battery life model was validated for accuracy. Using the data from the flight logs and the method for estimating current draw a percentage of the capacity drawn from 80% of the actual capacity was calculated. This was then multiplied to 80% of the actual voltage to estimate the voltage drop. The estimated voltage drop was then compared to the actual voltage drop and the results can be seen in Table 11. The estimated values are relatively accurate. Underestimates have less than a 10% error and overestimates still validate that the model proves there will be sufficient battery life. Errors in this can come from the</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;cell checkers inability to read to the hundredths place</span><span class="c10">.</span></p><p class="c24 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span class="c10">Table 9. Flight test logs and theoretical voltage drop </span></p><p class="c47 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.50px; height: 91.52px;"><img alt="" src="images/image26.png" style="width: 624.50px; height: 91.52px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c47 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.ehf4qhcxfddb"><span class="c28 c21">3.2.2 LiDAR [DH, GH]</span></h3><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">After the LiDAR was successfully installed into the aircraft, multiple test flights were done to confirm its functionality. In general, without the payload, the LiDAR was accurate and useful for its ability to &nbsp;provide the flight controller with highly accurate altitude data. With the payload however, there were multiple issues with it maintaining accurate data due to the sway of the paracord going into the beam of the LiDAR. UHDT did not necessarily need highly accurate altitude data unless UHDT were going out over the open ocean where wave heights can be unpredictable, or using autonomous functionality to align the drop. In the final configuration of the aircraft, the LiDAR was not being used. In future configurations of this system, it would be ideal to determine a way to use the LiDAR to obtain accurate altitude data for use in the open ocean. Additionally, it would be necessary to find solutions to potential LiDAR threats, whether intentional or not. Since LiDAR relies on measuring reflected light pulses, it is possible to easily disturb the LiDAR sensors, resulting in inaccurate measurements, particularly beyond visual line of sight (BVLOS).</span></p><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.3bczg1v4j5w"><span class="c28 c21">3.2.3 GNSS + RTK [DH]</span></h3><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">UHDT originally intended to use GNSS + RTK, &nbsp;and the centimeter level accuracy that it provides, to allow for the &nbsp;autonomous alignment and drop functionality to have the &nbsp;accuracy that is required. In the end, UHDT only used GNSS functionality of the HERE+ GNSS be here plus GPSS. Because UHDT does not require centimeter level accuracy, we did not use the RTK &nbsp;although it was tested successfully. The Here+ GNSS &nbsp;was more than accurate for what was required of UHDT. </span></p><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.8y4qi1kwvoor"><span class="c28 c21">3.2.4 Radio [DH]</span></h3><p class="c15 c47"><span class="c11">For testing the </span><span class="c11">900 MHz</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;fpv radios, UHDT only has anecdotal evidence provided by reviews of the module itself which corroborate its projected 15km range. UHDT was not able to get a full range 900 MHz test. Without a boat, it is difficult to test the radio under realistic conditions. However, we were able to test 900 MHz telemetry radios, which should have a similar range, at distances in excess of 10 km over water.</span></p><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c11">In order to confirm the theoretical 16.8 km range of the </span><span class="c11">433 MHz</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;telemetry and control radio, UHDT used the &ldquo;range test mode&rdquo; &nbsp;available via the Dragon link. &nbsp;This mode cuts the output power of the radio by 30 and roughly &nbsp;produces an estimate &nbsp;of the modules range. In &ldquo;range test mode&rdquo; UHDT was able to test the radio at a range of 300 m, this gives an estimated full range of 9 km. At 300 m the radio had 34% RSSI.</span></p><p class="c47 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.t4nqb1q2crth"><span>3.3</span><span class="c28 c21">&nbsp;Delivery</span></h2><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.jm5d2s3ctg4j"><span>3.3.1 Payload compartment [DD]</span></h3><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c11">The payload compartments most mission critical task is to complete MR-3, &ldquo;The UAS shall deliver the payload without damage to any component of the </span><span class="c11">UAS</span><span class="c11">&rdquo;. The payload compartment was able to to survive all water drops without damage. &nbsp;Although the compartment was never dropped from the aircraft while in flight loaded with the full 2.3 kg, our mission demonstrations consisted of the unloaded payload dropping from an altitude of roughly 100 ft into water. Through analysis it can be shown that successfully surviving impact from 100 ft without weight corresponds to surviving our full mission of dropping from 10 m into the ocean with a full weight payload. &nbsp;Although the payload is unweighted, dropping from three times the height increases its impact velocity and thus impact force. Using free fall drag Equations 1- 4 with mass of the payload compartment as 1.465 kg, density of air as 1.225 </span><img src="images/image10.png"><span class="c11">, C</span><span class="c11 c67">d</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;of an ogive as 0.295, and frontal area as 0.0331 m</span><span class="c11 c91">2</span><span class="c10">, free fall velocity from 100 ft is 23.005 m/s. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><img src="images/image11.png"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(4)</span></p><p class="c22 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><img src="images/image12.png"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(5)</span></p><p class="c22 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><img><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(6)</span></p><p class="c22 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><img><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(7)&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">Taking into account an average ground speed of 16 m/s, resultant impact velocity is 28.018 m/s. Using Equation 5 to calculate impact force, the impact force dropped while flying at 100 ft without the 2.3 kg weighted payload is 1431.57 N. &nbsp;The impact force with the 2.3 kg weighted payload dropped while flying at 10 m &nbsp;is 1151.76 N. &nbsp;The impact force applied to the payload during our demonstration and tests exceed that what we would experience on a flight mission out at sea, validating our impact model and completing MR-3.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><img src="images/image13.png"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(8)</span></p><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.c3n42ysuz0iv"><span class="c28 c21">3.3.2 Trajectory [AH]</span></h3><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">Videos and flight test logs from the April 17, 2019 flight test were used to validate trajectory predictions, where screenshots of the test drop from the point of release to the point of impact were stitched together (see Fig 19).</span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.00px; height: 302.46px;"><img alt="" src="images/image59.png" style="width: 624.00px; height: 302.46px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span class="c10">Figure 19. Screenshots of the test drop showing the trajectory path of the payload compartment and its corresponding measurements. The experimental path is shown in red and the modeled path is shown in blue.</span></p><p class="c24 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">The drop altitude, wind conditions, and speed of the UAS at the time of the drop were obtained from the flight logs and paired with the corresponding measurements to calculate the horizontal length travelled by the payload compartment (see Table 10).</span></p><p class="c47 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span class="c10">Table 10. Table showing flight parameters taken from the flight log, experimental horizontal distance travelled, and the predicted horizontal distance travelled from the trajectory model.</span></p><a id="t.8cb55e2ad3dfa8d891061a77ffacc881ca362711"></a><a id="t.11"></a><table class="c65"><tbody><tr class="c8"><td class="c93" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c11">V</span><span class="c11 c67">UAS</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;[m/s]</span></p></td><td class="c112" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c11">m</span><span class="c11 c67">payload</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;[g]</span></p></td><td class="c128" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Altitude [m]</span></p></td><td class="c92" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Scaled Altitude [cm]</span></p></td><td class="c93" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Scaled Horizontal [cm]</span></p></td><td class="c134" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Experimental Horizontal [m]</span></p></td><td class="c66" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Predicted Horizontal [m]</span></p></td><td class="c89" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">Error [%]</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c93" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">12</span></p></td><td class="c112" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">1600</span></p></td><td class="c128" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">3</span></p></td><td class="c92" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">4.2</span></p></td><td class="c93" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">10.1</span></p></td><td class="c134" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><img src="images/image14.png"><span class="c10">7.214</span></p></td><td class="c66" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">6.127</span></p></td><td class="c89" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17"><span class="c10">17.7</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c47 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">Although a percent error of 17.7 seems high, there were a lot of areas where approximations had to be made. For example, the wind conditions were modeled as a constant speed of 5 m/s instead of the actual variable winds seen at the time of the drop. Another area of approximation was the scaling used on the screenshots of the video. Since the flight test videos were not stationary, the screenshots taken during the test drop were at differing angles and zooms, making it difficult to accurately measure the experimental horizontal distance travelled by the payload compartment. Thus, all things considered, the trajectory model closely predicts the actual drop of the payload compartment, aiding in accurately delivering the payload to the maritime vessel.</span></p><p class="c47 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.qvtjyu21b9j4"><span class="c28 c21">3.3.3 Servo-Pushrod [AH]</span></h3><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">The servo-pushrod was designed to fulfill MR-1, where the delivery system needed to be able to both hold and release a payload of 2.3 kg. To account for dynamic loading during flight, added air resistance, tension from the paracord, and a higher factor of safety, the delivery subsystem aimed to create a system capable of holding and releasing at least 5 kg. Bench testing the final design consisted of multiple drops with a static load of 11.5 lbs, with the plane oriented at differing angles to mimic all possible conditions during a flight test. The drop system was able to hold and release the 11.5 lbs at the differing angles with a 100% success rate, exceeding the 5 kg goal set by the delivery subsystem. Finally, during the final flight test on May 1, 2019, the system successfully held and released the 5 lb payload.</span></p><p class="c47 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><h1 class="c40 c23" id="h.ky4ukl9go98p"><span>4.0</span><span>&nbsp;Safety, Risks, &amp; Mitigations </span></h1><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.719k6aft60xv"><span>4</span><span>.1 Developmental Risks &amp; Mitigations [KT]</span></h2><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">Developmental risks considered are time, cost, and performance. The risk of not having enough time or funds to complete the project poses a threat to any developmental process. Performance risks occur if the system is not well designed which may be consequent from poor requirement developments. </span></p><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">To mitigate the risk of time, a Gantt chart was used to keep track of top-level tasks for every subsystem. Trello was used in parallel to assign detailed tasks to each member with due dates and completion status. The overall Gantt chart was reviewed periodically and revised to have a better estimate on the status of the team and the remaining buffer time to delegate mission-critical tasks.</span></p><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">To mitigate the risk of cost, a budget estimate was made at every design gate. The Preliminary Design Review (PDR) budget was based on UHDT 2017-2018 team&rsquo;s cost of parts and equipment. The Critical Design Review (CDR) budget was based on online prices for parts expected to be used in the CDR design. The project would be fully funded by ARL but additional finance from the Mechanical Engineering (ME) funds and VIP funds were also obtained for emergency purposes. </span></p><p class="c47 c15"><span class="c10">To mitigate the risk of performance, the created MRs and FRs were reviewed at each design gate to ensure that they captured the full intent of the mission at hand. A requirements verification matrix was utilized, and all requirements were reviewed to see if they were specific, measurable, actionable, realistic, testable, and traceable. At each design gate, the proposed design was also reviewed by UHDT advisors and ARL.</span></p><p class="c47 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.45b3i7xiwvyw"><span>4</span><span class="c28 c21">.2 Mission Risks &amp; Mitigations [KT]</span></h2><p class="c22"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To determine risks that are critical to the project development and mission, a risk matrix was utilized. Because this is a new project the levels of likelihood are unknown as prior data is not available. Thus a risk matrix utilizing 4 levels of risk was chosen. The levels being green for low, yellow for medium, orange for high, and red for critical.</span></p><p class="c22 c64"><span class="c11">The mission risks for each subsystem are denoted by the first letter of the subsystem: D for delivery, A for aircraft, and E for electronics. The mission risks for the delivery subsystem are: D1: Failure to release and deploy payload or retrieval cord, D2: Failure to drop payload within range of maritime platform, and D3: Failure to protect UAV from a tangled cord. The mission risks for the aircraft subsystem are: A1: Failure to takeoff, A2: Loss of control of the aircraft while in flight, and A3: Not enough pitch control from paracord drag. The mission risks for the electronics subsystem are: E1: Electronics failure, E2: Failure to detect the delivery target, and E3: Failure to detect and troubleshoot issues. The risks were placed in </span><span class="c11">Table</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;11</span><span class="c11">, the risk matrix, depending on its likelihood and consequence.</span></p><p class="c9 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c6"><span class="c10">Table 11. Risk Matrix</span></p><a id="t.7a2a8ba7b4307c092653adaa3772dd929338c3f8"></a><a id="t.12"></a><table class="c73"><tbody><tr class="c79"><td class="c133" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c118" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c121" colspan="5" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">Consequences</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c79"><td class="c99" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c39" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td></tr><tr class="c79"><td class="c39" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">Likely</span></p></td><td class="c115" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c84" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c80" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">A3</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c79"><td class="c39" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">Possible</span></p></td><td class="c95" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c27 c135" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c108" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">A1|D2</span></p></td><td class="c84" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">E2|D3</span></p></td><td class="c80" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td></tr><tr class="c79"><td class="c39" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">Unlikely</span></p></td><td class="c95" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c27 c135" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c108" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c69" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">E3</span></p></td><td class="c90" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">A2|E1</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c79"><td class="c39" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6"><span class="c10">Rare</span></p></td><td class="c95" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c27 c130" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c108" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c69" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td><td class="c90" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c6 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c47 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c47 c23"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To mitigate these risks a risk burn down method was utilized. When each of these mitigations methods are</span></p><p class="c47 c23"><span class="c10">utilized it will reduce the likelihood of the risk, subsequently reducing the overall risk. </span></p><p class="c31 c23"><span class="c10">&nbsp;The mitigations for the delivery subsystem are: D1: Calculate torque needed for payload weight (payload compartment weight included) and static test of servo drop before integration; D2: Test accuracy of drop and validate trajectory model and practice manual drop using FPV; D3: Ensure cord drops smoothly and calculate/measure the time to unravel and angle of drag and ensure mission area has no tall obstacles or if there is pay close attention to the flight plan. </span></p><p class="c31 c23"><span class="c10">&nbsp;The mitigations for the aircraft subsystem are: A1: Properly size the propulsion system, do preflight and safety checklists, and do static thrust test with different props and motors to validate thrust; A2: Do preflight and safety checklists, make sure that all components are in good working condition, and do post-flight checks and repair as necessary; A3: Redesign moment and attachment point, and validate pitch control on new MTD, move attachment point behind AC to account for pitch control, and validate maximum allowable rope length to drag before drag moment exceeds pitch control.</span></p><p class="c23 c31"><span class="c10">The mitigations for the electronics subsystem are: E1: Test individual electronics before integration and test entire electronics systems before integration, and validate sensor data output during pre-flight check tests; E2: Added the use of boat GPS location and LiDAR and FPV and pilot visual will always be available; E3: Have one person during flight tests responsible for announcing necessary flight data to the pilot in command and do post-flight check to make sure no damage to electronic components.</span></p><p class="c31 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><h1 class="c40 c23" id="h.oi08j09poouo"><span>5.0</span><span>&nbsp;Technical Suggestions for Improvement</span></h1><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.qf1vxn5049nj"><span>5.1 Aircraft [CR, JC]</span></h2><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">There is not much to modify on the MTDs design. In terms of stability, the MTD covers directional stability greatly due to the size of its vertical stabilizers. The MTD&rsquo;s high wing configuration helps the aircraft in terms of lateral stability. And for longitudinal stability, as long as the CG lies within the static margin the MTD should have good pitch stability.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">For missions requiring more speed and more maneuverability, the structural integrity of the wings have to be reinforced. In missions such as this, the wing loading will increase due to the change of the load factor for every maneuver that the aircraft will take. To remedy this, an extra hole could be drilled and a spar could be inserted to prevent wing breakage and allow the plane to perform more intense maneuvers.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">If the payload requirement is increased while keeping the same size of the payload compartment, there are two possible options to help accomplish the mission. Due to the mass increase, hand launch takeoffs might no longer become an option as there will be a tendency to inadvertently hit the empennage. Because of this, the aircraft might always start at a high negative pitch angle and therefore making it almost impossible for takeoff. One option to make the mission still possible will be the addition of a landing gear. This will mean that the aircraft will have to start from the ground during takeoff. Possible drawbacks for this option is the integration of the landing gears and a long runway for takeoff, as well as added weight. Another option that can be taken is to make a mechanical launcher. This way the aircraft will always have a positive pitch during takeoff.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">A final consideration for improvement or scalability of the system would be to use a bigger plane. The advantage of this would be more thrust, a higher payload capacity, longer flight distance, and ease of taking off. Some disadvantages are higher cost for purchasing a new one and more expensive repairs. Since the mission only called for a 2.3 kg payload, perhaps a bigger plane wouldn&rsquo;t be needed. To reduce the costs and time of repairs - especially during critical field test time, commonly damaged areas such as the tail and the nose can be reinforced with fiberglass. These reinforcements can be easily transferred from plane to plane to reduce the chances of major damages.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.mi3x6mudd8sw"><span>5.2</span><span class="c28 c21">&nbsp;Electronics</span></h2><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.pbjwfb2guq0u"><span class="c28 c21">5.2.1 Object Detection [MI]</span></h3><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">The next step would be to integrate object detection to autonomously direct the UAS to the maritime platform. This can be done using the field of view of the onboard camera. Using the field of view of the camera, a Python script can be written to determine the current trajectory of the </span><span class="c11">UAS</span><span class="c11">. This information would be used to determine whether or not the course needs to be adjusted. If the course of the </span><span class="c11">UAS</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;needed to be adjusted, it can be done through Mission Planner. Mission Planner has the ability to run Python scripts. So through Mission Planner, a Python script could be injected to allow the UAS to autonomously adjust its course. Another thing that can be improved upon in the future is the speed of the object detection model. Currently the model runs at a real time speed of approximately 1 frame per second. This processing speed is unideal given the UAS will be traveling at a speed of approximately 20 meters per second. Therefore, it would be beneficial to either find a faster model or optimize the current model. </span></p><p class="c9 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.mf1sg3nv86tx"><span class="c28 c21">5.2.2 Battery [DH]</span></h3><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">&nbsp;Possible integration of lithium ion batteries for higher efficiency can be considered for future teams. Lithium ion batteries are in general more energy dense than lithium polymer batteries. For this discussion, the Panasonic 18650 cells will be used as a baseline. We currently use a 16 Ah lithium polymer battery weighing 1300g. To achieve the same capacity and voltage, we would need 20 lithium ion cells, 4S5P, each with 3.4 Ah weighing a mere 900g. This, at face value sounds incredible. We could either increase our capacity dramatically or make room for other things in our weight budget. The downside to lithium ion batteries are their poor discharge capability. For batteries, a C rating is used to denote its discharge capability. For example, the 16 Ah batteries that UHDT uses for its flights has a C rating of 10 C. With a C rating of 10, and an amp rating of 16, the </span><span class="c11">instantaneous</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;discharge capability can be calculated to be 160 amps, or 10 C * 16 Ah. &nbsp; As a maximum, UHDT only uses about 100 A at any single time, specifically during takeoffs. &nbsp;The downside to lithium ion cells is their abysmal 1 C rating. This means that UHDT would need a total of 140 cells, weighing 6300g to match the discharge rate required for the planes to take off.</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">The sweet spot would be somewhere in between, possibly even using some of each with lithium ion and lithium polymer. If we took 20 lithium-ion cells required to match the capacity of our lithium polymer, it&#39;s discharge capability would be 17 A. While cruising, our system draws anywhere between 10 A to 30 A depending on the conditions. Although lithium ions are not sufficient on their own. If a system was put in place that could charge a lithium polymer battery, possibly as small as 1.8 Ah at 100 C, it could allow the system to be overall both more energy dense and still capable of delivering the amperage required for takeoff. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.yniucppj6ary"><span>5.3</span><span class="c28 c21">&nbsp;Delivery</span></h2><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.wlp51xje3u62"><span class="c28 c21">5.3.1 Payload Compartment [DD]</span></h3><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">To improve the payload compartment, it could be 3D printed out of ABS as originally planned because ABS has a higher yield strength and is less dense. &nbsp;A 3D printer with high enough nozzle temperature would have to be used. &nbsp;If &nbsp; size specifications of the payload were given, the compartment could have been reduced in size to reduce drag. Also to reduce drag, a smooth surface finish could be applied. Lastly, fins could have been used on the compartment to insure a nose down water impact and lessen the impact force. &nbsp;Testing would have to be done to ensure the fins would not break off in the water.</span></p><p class="c1"><span class="c10"></span></p><h2 class="c63 c23" id="h.f0d4d0fjnax7"><span class="c28 c21">5.4 Project Management and Systems Engineering</span></h2><h3 class="c63 c23" id="h.m3m8ceqmgttf"><span class="c28 c21">5.4.1 Flight Test Procedures [KT, KR]</span></h3><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">Flight test procedures should have been much more rigorous from the very beginning. The discrepancies may have been due to the fact that the team was working on a new system but was utilizing the previous year&rsquo;s &nbsp;checklist. Roles should also be assigned for every flight test as it increases productivity and sense of responsibility to all members present. Pre-departure and pre-flight checklists should be modified every year to check if older check sheets are still relevant and if new components are introduced they should be included to the check sheets. More individuals should also be tasked with being able to read the flight logs for post flight analysis and validation of models.</span></p><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c10">Due to the increased weight, the UAV needed to consistently be launched at a slightly positive pitch angle. Failure to do so at full weight would cause it to lose altitude to the point where the payload was in danger of striking the ground, putting the whole system at risk. In previous years the UAV was light and powerful enough that the initial angle of attack was a less important for a successful takeoff. Future teams should take this into consideration when hand launching the UAV.</span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><h3 class="c23 c63" id="h.yq01kf1q9381"><span class="c28 c21">5.4.2 Risk Mitigation [KT]</span></h3><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c11">Although the risks and risk mitigation was thought out and executed, UHDT-M faced some of the said risks during testing. Risks: D1, D3, A1, A2, E1 were all encountered during the testing phase. There were many aspects of the mitigation plan which overlooked certain aspects or was not rigorous enough to increase the rate of detection. Although a failure mode &amp; effects analysis (FMEA) was conducted for the upper level mission, it was never done for the individual risks which were mentioned. Utilizing proper use of a risk priority number (RPN) would have been very helpful and may have shown red flags in some risks which would have prompted for a more rigorous mitigation method.</span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><h1 class="c40 c23" id="h.j7cqgidx2udh"><span>6.0</span><span class="c28 c21">&nbsp;Final Budget and Schedule</span></h1><h2 class="c40 c23" id="h.y1xe5jy7ah8v"><span class="c28 c21">6.1 Budget [DD]</span></h2><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c11">Table 12 shows how UHDT-M budgeted a total project cost of </span><span class="c11">$</span><span class="c11">7,359.44 consisting of components and materials needed for each subsystem. Figure 19 is the expense chart which details what percentage of the budget was allocated to each of the three subsystems. At PDR, our proposed budget including 10% buffer was $7,755. At CDR this was reduced to $5,629 once more information on the items needed was available. This did not include enough spare items which proved crucial during testing. At DDMR, the proposed budget rose back to $7,906, and for the Midterm report was $7,604.67, which was the most accurate budget, including any spares. Funding for the project came from ARL and the VIP program. ARL supplied $6,801.97 of funds, which went toward purchasing the majority of all the aircraft, electronics, delivery, and prototyping supplies, along with multiple spares. &nbsp;The maximum budget given to UHDT-M by ARL was $7,000. From the VIP funds, $487.97 out of the $750 have been used. The team did not use the available $1,000 from the Mechanical Engineering department. The remaining cost of the project is due to team member purchase of the paracord. </span></p><p class="c1 c64"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c34 c23"><span class="c45">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span class="c11">Table 12. </span><span class="c10">Project Budget</span></p><p class="c34 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp; </span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 239.84px; height: 312.28px;"><img alt="" src="images/image56.png" style="width: 239.84px; height: 315.11px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: -1.42px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span class="c11">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; </span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 334.11px; height: 207.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image69.png" style="width: 334.46px; height: 207.50px; margin-left: -0.17px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span class="c45">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span class="c11">Figure 20. </span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">Project Expense Chart</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><h2 class="c23 c40" id="h.4o48gtrs85cx"><span>6.2</span><span class="c28 c21">&nbsp;Schedule [LP]</span></h2><p class="c24 c15"><span class="c11">The timeline for UHDT-M takes place during the UH 2018-19 AY (August 2018 - May 2019) and included 5 weeks of buffer time of which all were used. The timeline was designed to end in May prior to the completion of the Spring 2019 semester. The UHDT-M team consists of 15 members of which 10 are senior class members and the remaining 5 are underclassmen. Currently, 10 members are from a mechanical engineering discipline, and 5 members from an electrical engineering discipline. With this amount of members from different disciplines, not only the scheduling of key subsystem tasks and integration is of importance, but time management </span><span class="c11">of those tasks also play a key role. &nbsp;The updated gantt chart for UHDT-M academic year is shown in </span><span class="c11">Figure 20. For optimal readability, the gantt chart has been broken into parts, thus allowing sections to be viewed on a larger scale. &nbsp;</span><span class="c10">It should be noted that while the gantt chart was followed in general to ensure all necessary tasks were completed, unforeseen circumstances sometimes required a deviation from the gantt chart. Some subsystems were put on hold for certain tasks as other problems or circumstances from other subsystems were being solved. Also, due to multiple crashes throughout the semester, much time was diverted toward the reassembly of the damaged UAV, or new assembly of brand new airframes and components. Furthermore, UHDT-M experienced many delays in items that were ordered off island. Being located in Hawaii sometimes poses a unique problem with not only finding vendors who ship to this location, but also in receiving orders within a small time frame. Therefore, tolerances were allotted with regard to the gantt chart, and schedules for all subsystems were not held strictly to plan.</span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 606.05px; height: 301.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image40.jpg" style="width: 674.10px; height: 321.96px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: -20.46px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 607.00px; height: 305.01px;"><img alt="" src="images/image16.jpg" style="width: 675.17px; height: 324.55px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: -19.54px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c24 c15"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 601.00px; height: 351.16px;"><img alt="" src="images/image36.jpg" style="width: 662.59px; height: 372.31px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: -21.15px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c24 c23"><span class="c10">Figure 21. Gantt chart for the UHDT-M academic year</span></p><p class="c47 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><h1 class="c40 c23" id="h.x7khaul6aagu"><span>7.0</span><span class="c28 c21">&nbsp;Conclusion [LP]</span></h1><p class="c22 c15"><span class="c11">The 2018-2019 AY serves as the fifth year of UHDT&rsquo;s venture in advancing UAS technologies and its fourth consecutive entry in the annual AUVSI Seafarer SUAS competition. However, UHDT-M serves as the first year that an extension of this team was formed and a collaborative project with ARL was created. UHDT-M aims to fulfill its mission to</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">produce a UAV capable of transporting a payload to a maritime platform. From this mission statement, a set of MRs, as well as a set of FRs were developed. These requirements guide all subsystems to fulfill UHDT-M&rsquo;s current mission statement.</span><span class="c116">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">Currently, UHDT-M has satisfied all mission requirements. Mission requirement 1: The UAS shall be capable of flight with a 2.3 kg payload, mission requirement 3: The UAS shall deliver the payload without damage to any component of the </span><span class="c11">UAS</span><span class="c11">, were both physically met during flight with the UAV, and mission constraint: The UAS shall adhere to laws set by the government. Mission requirement 2</span><span class="c11">: The UAS shall have the ability to reach the maritime platform 8 km offshore, was met analytically. Due to FAA rules and regulations, mission requirement 2 could not be met physically, and therefore the capability of the UAV to meet mission requirement 2 had to be shown through calculations. While using a systems engineering approach it is believed the implementation of s</span><span class="c11">emi-autonomous flight, object detection, and air delivery</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;will result in a precise and accurate air delivery system called Moltres. </span></p><hr style="page-break-before:always;display:none;"><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><h1 class="c63 c23" id="h.z73vsas1uwrn"><span class="c28 c21">References</span></h1><p class="c34 c23"><span class="c11">[1]</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">&ldquo;Wilhelmsen Launches Delivery Drone Service at Nor-Shipping.&rdquo; </span><span class="c0">The Maritime Executive</span><span class="c10">, maritime-</span></p><p class="c34 c23 c61 c120"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;executive.com/article/wss-ships-agency-launches-delivery-drone-service.</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">[2] </span><span class="c11">&nbsp;&ldquo;Special Delivery: Aerial Drones Deliver Shore-to-Ship.&rdquo; </span><span class="c0">MarineLink</span><span class="c10">, Maritime Activity Reports, Inc., 5 June </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">2018, </span><span class="c11"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=http://www.marinelink.com/news/special-delivery-aerial-drones-deliver-438250&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084594000">www.marinelink.com/news/special-delivery-aerial-drones-deliver-438250</a></span><span class="c10">.</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">[3] &ldquo;Airbus Completes First Flight Demonstration for Its Commercial Parcel Delivery Drone &#39;Skyways&#39;.&rdquo; </span><span class="c0">Airbus</span><span class="c10">, </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">www.airbus.com/newsroom/press-releases/en/2018/02/airbus-Completes-first-</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">flight-demonstration-for-its-commercial-p.html.</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">[4] Shankland, S. Plane documentation</span><span class="c11">. [online] &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Ardupilot.org. Available at: http://ardupilot.org/plane/docs/automatic-tuning-with-autotune.html [Accessed 9 Mar. 2019].</span><hr style="page-break-before:always;display:none;"></p><h1 class="c63 c23" id="h.gjpz6fq8p62m"><span>Appendix</span></h1><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">Appendix A: System Specifications Document [KT]</span></p><ol class="c33 lst-kix_z5on9tefouvx-0 start" start="1"><li class="c12 c9"><span class="c10">Mission Requirements</span></li></ol><ol class="c33 lst-kix_z5on9tefouvx-1 start" start="1"><li class="c9 c78 c23"><span class="c10">The Mission Statement is satisfied when various objectives are accomplished. These objectives are designated as mission requirements (MR). The MRs shown in Table A-1 are the VIP UHDT Maritime&rsquo;s success criteria for AY 2018-2019. Each Mission Requirement is fulfilled by functional requirements (FR) created by each individual subsystem. A detailed description of the FR that satisfy each MR is found in Table A-2.</span></li></ol><p class="c6 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span class="c10">Table A-1: Description of Mission Requirements</span></p><a id="t.faf35b2d33dd901296d50783cbbceb59d826c542"></a><a id="t.13"></a><table class="c65"><tbody><tr class="c8"><td class="c35" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p
