[FAA]</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><hr style="page-break-before:always;display:none;"><p class="c6 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span class="c10">Table A-5: Description of Electronics/Communications Subsystem Functional Requirements</span></p><a id="t.7dafdb0ec15571e7ebf7a2bd8fb4db523c03fc31"></a><a id="t.17"></a><table class="c65"><tbody><tr class="c8"><td class="c35" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c30"><span class="c28 c21">Functional Requirement</span></p></td><td class="c54" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c17 c64"><span class="c28 c21">Description</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c79"><td class="c50" colspan="2" rowspan="1"><p class="c17 c64"><span class="c28 c21">Electronics/Communications</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c35" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c30"><span class="c10">FR-ES-1</span></p></td><td class="c54" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c9"><span class="c10">The UAV shall be able to identify the target for the delivery method. [Self-imposed]</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c35" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c30"><span class="c10">FR-ES-2</span></p></td><td class="c54" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c9"><span class="c10">The UAV shall have sufficient battery power to fly out 8 km (5 miles) and deliver payload. [ARL]</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c35" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c30"><span class="c10">FR-ES-3</span></p></td><td class="c54" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c9"><span class="c10">The UAV shall maintain operational communications with GCS, R/C, FPV, and provide telemetry data to the GCS up to a 8 km (5 mile) range. [ARL]</span></p></td></tr><tr class="c8"><td class="c35" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c30"><span class="c10">FR-ES-4</span></p></td><td class="c54" colspan="1" rowspan="1"><p class="c9"><span class="c10">The UAV shall have autonomous RTH/RTL capabilities. [Self-imposed]</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Appendix B: Organizational Chart</span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span class="c10">Figure B-1. Team Organizational Chart</span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 541.00px; height: 324.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image74.jpg" style="width: 541.97px; height: 324.00px; margin-left: -0.49px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><hr style="page-break-before:always;display:none;"><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c34 c23"><span class="c45">Appendix C:</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;New Aircraft Tuning SOP [DH]</span></p><p class="c9"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Due to a complete mission failure crash, the SOP for new aircraft tuning needs to be updated. Until now the SOP for aircraft tuning utilized the Ardupilot &ldquo;auto-tune&rdquo; function. &nbsp;This function puts the plane into a highly unstable and maneuverable tune and asks the pilot to force the plane into oscillations. The software alters the gain values on all axes until the aircraft is stable. </span></p><p class="c9 c64"><span class="c10">After the auto-tune is completed, the plane is put through a series of extreme maneuvers in fly-by-wire mode. The maneuvers made are more extreme than those of which the plane will ever experience in autonomous mode. If there are any oscillations on any axes then that axis will be tuned manually. The manual tuning mainly involves increasing the D value on the PID controller and dampening the inputs until there are absolutely no oscillations. </span></p><p class="c9 c64"><span class="c10">In this specific situation, the aircraft had been put into auto-tune mode for an unsatisfactory amount of time. The aircraft was initially put into an auto-tune state, and all axes were pushed to their extreme. Then, after the drop rope was added to the aircraft, the aircraft was put into the second round of auto-tune mode where the aircraft was unsatisfactorily pushed to its extreme on the pitch axis. Because of this, after the aircraft was landed, the maximum speed of the aircraft and the maximum climb rate of the aircraft were substantially lower than is viable. Due to this, when UHDT attempted to take off post tune flight, the aircraft was unable to climb given that its maximum speed on climb out was lower than the speed necessary to climb.</span></p><p class="c9 c64"><span class="c10">&nbsp;In the future, the aircraft needs to be held in its auto-tune function until all axes have been calibrated. Each axis needs to be pushed to its limits at least 20 times[13] in the auto-tune mode at both full throttle and zero throttle. Then, and only then can the first step of aircraft tune be completed. </span></p><p class="c9 c64 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c34 c23"><span class="c45">Appendix D:</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;Object Detection Tutorial [MI]</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Introduction:</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">The goal of the object detection is to meet the functional requirement of the Unmanned Aircraft System (UAS) being able to identify a maritime target for the delivery method. Object detection became necessary when unforeseen problems were encountered when testing the Infrared (IR) Lock sensor. When testing the IR Lock sensor, it was discovered that the effective range would be too low for its desired use. The range of the sensor was determined to be approximately 20 meters. Given that the UAS will be flying at approximately 20 meters per second, the sensor would only allow for approximately one second to detect the boat and for the payload to be dropped. This is clearly infeasible, so another approach will be needed. Another problem with the IR Lock sensor discovered during testing was that it was too sensitive to sunlight and would be ineffective for the purpose of the mission. With object detection, we believe we will be able to increase the range and accuracy for detecting the maritime platform. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">Two approaches to object detection will be covered in this tutorial. The first will be to take the input of a live stream from a camera onboard the UAS and send it to a computer on the ground which will process the video to detect the boat. The second will be to use a Raspberry Pi Camera onboard the </span><span class="c11">UAS</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;so that the processing of the video can be done onboard the </span><span class="c11">UAS</span><span class="c10">. The benefit of the first approach is that a computer will be able to process the video much faster than a Raspberry Pi. However, the video will first need to be sent from the feed onboard the UAS to the computer on the ground which will cause a delay. Both approaches are similar, but you will need to determine which approach is most ideal for the task. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">A brief description of object detection is a computer technology associated with image processing and computer vision used to detect instances of different classes of objects in pictures and videos. The two main categories of object detection are machine-learning and deep-learning. The method we chose to work with was the deep-learning approach, in particular, an approach called You Only Look Once (YOLO). YOLO is a real-time object detection system that is fast and accurate. The way YOLO works is by dividing an image into regions and predicts probabilities in each region. The predicted probabilities are used to weight the predictions of the bounding boxes. An advantage of using YOLO is that it is trained on the Common Objects in Context (COCO) dataset. The COCO dataset includes people, cars, animals, household objects, and much more. But most importantly for our application, COCO is trained on boats. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">A disadvantage of using YOLO object detection is that it can struggle to detect smaller objects as well as objects grouped closely together. However, in our application, we don&rsquo;t have to worry about objects being grouped closely together since the boat will be in the middle of the ocean. Therefore, the many advantages of using YOLO definitely outweigh the drawbacks. In our application, we don&rsquo;t have to worry about objects being grouped closely together </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">The implementation of the YOLO object detection will be done using the software OpenCV and Tensorflow. The process for installing OpenCV and Tensorflow will be covered in the section below.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Installation Process on macOS:</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">This section will go over the installation process for the software that is necessary for object detection on macOS. The tutorial will cover the installation of Python, OpenCV, and Tensorflow. The first installation that will be covered is Python. Python is a programming language commonly used in applications like object detection. To download Python, the first step is to download the Python 3 installer. This can be done using the download page of python.org. Although there are newer versions available, be sure to download </span><span class="c45">Python 3.6 </span><span class="c10">or older. The reason this is necessary will be explained later in the tutorial. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">The best way to install Python on </span><span class="c11">macOS</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;is to use a package manager called homebrew. However, homebrew is dependant on a software package called XCode. So, the first command you will need to run in the Terminal is </span><span class="c45">xcode-select --</span><span class="c45">installx</span><span class="c45">.</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;After XCode is installed, you can install homebrew running the command</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;/usr/bin/ruby -e &quot;$(curl -fsSL </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://raw.githubusercontent.com/Homebrew/install/master/install&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084639000">https://raw.githubusercontent.com/Homebrew/install/master/install</a></span><span class="c45">)&quot;</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;in the Terminal. To verify that homebrew is properly installed, run the command </span><span class="c45">brew doctor</span><span class="c11">. If homebrew is installed properly you should see the output &ldquo;your system is ready to brew.&rdquo; Be sure to update your ~/.bash_profile, failure to do so can cause errors later on. &nbsp;You can update your ~/.bash_profile by opening it with a text editor, I prefer Atom. &nbsp;After opening the ~/.bash_profile, add </span><span class="c38">export PATH=/usr/local/bin:$PATH</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">to the bottom of the file. Then run the command </span><span class="c45">source ~/.bash_profile</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">to save the changes. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">Finally, you can install Python using the command </span><span class="c45">brew install python3</span><span class="c11">. To verify that Python was installed successfully, run the command </span><span class="c45">python3 --version</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;and the version of Python that is currently installed should be output in the command window. </span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Next, we will cover the installation process for OpenCV. OpenCV is a computer vision library primarily used for real-time applications. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">Before beginning the installation process, verify you have version 3.6 of Python. OpenCV is not supported by Python 3.7, so it is very important that you confirm you have the proper version. If you currently have Python 3.7, you will need to uninstall it and reinstall Python 3.6. To uninstall Python on a Mac there are multiple options. The first is to open the applications folder, select the Python folder, move it to the trash, and empty the trash. However, you will then need to find and remove any other files Python created. The next option is to use the command line and run the command </span><span class="c45">sudo rm -rf /Applications/Python\ 3.7/. </span><span class="c11">The last option is to download an </span><span class="c11">app cleaner</span><span class="c10">/uninstaller from the App store.</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">To install </span><span class="c10">OpenCV on macOS you will first need to make sure you have Xcode and homebrew installed. If you do not, the installation process for them can be found above in the Python installation process section. After verifying you have Xcode and homebrew installed, you can begin to install the dependencies for OpenCV. These packages will be used to do things like build and compile OpenCV, I/O operations, or for optimization. To install these dependencies, run the commands shown below from the command line. </span></p><p class="c9 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 348.00px; height: 65.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image75.png" style="width: 348.00px; height: 65.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c13"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">Another useful tool to install is wget, which allows you to download files from the internet via the command line. You can install wget by running the command </span><span class="c45">brew install wge</span><span class="c11">t. You can now take advantage of the wget command to install a Python package manager called pip. To do so, run the command </span><span class="c45">wget </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://bootstrap.pypa.io/get-pip.py&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084641000">https://bootstrap.pypa.io/get-pip.py</a></span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">followed by </span><span class="c45">sudo python3 get-pip.py</span><span class="c0">. &nbsp;</span><span class="c11">Now that you have pip installed, you can install a virtual environment to manage your Python projects. It is recommended, but not necessary. The benefit of using a virtual machine is so that when working on multiple projects, each project&#39;s dependencies are independent of each other. The steps to do install the virtual manager are to first run the command </span><span class="c45">sudo pip3 install virtualenv virtualenvwrapper </span><span class="c0">.</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;Then, you will need to again modify your bash profile. You can do so by either using a text editor or directly from the command line. If you chose to edit the </span><span class="c11">bash profile</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;using a text editor, append the following lines</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">shown below to the end of the file and save the changes. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 434.50px; height: 58.56px;"><img alt="" src="images/image19.png" style="width: 434.50px; height: 58.56px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">If you choose to edit the </span><span class="c11">bash profile</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;from the command line, execute the commands shown below.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 567.00px; height: 71.71px;"><img alt="" src="images/image66.png" style="width: 567.00px; height: 71.71px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">Now that you have installed a virtual environment, you can use it for OpenCV. Run the command </span><span class="c45">mkvirtualenv cv -p python3</span><span class="c0">, </span><span class="c11">which</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">will create a virtual environment named &ldquo;cv.&rdquo; To work in the virtual environment, use the command </span><span class="c45">workon cv</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">Now that you are in the virtual environment, the next step is to install the Python library NumPy, which can be done using the command </span><span class="c45">pip3 install numpy</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">You are now ready to compile OpenCV. While it is possible to compile OpenCV using package managers such as homebrew or pip, the recommended way to do so is to compile OpenCV by source. Although compiling OpenCV by source is much more tedious, it is the most proper and robust way of doing so. First, you will need to download the opencv and opencv_contrib zip files. This can be done using the commands </span><span class="c28 c21">wget -O opencv.zip https://github.com/opencv/opencv/archive/4.0.0.zip</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">wget -O opencv_contrib.zip </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/opencv/opencv_contrib/archive/4.0.0.zip&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084643000">https://github.com/opencv/opencv_contrib/archive/4.0.0.zip</a></span><span class="c45">.</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">To unzip the files, use the commands </span><span class="c28 c21">unzip opencv.zip</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">&nbsp;unzip opencv_contrib.zip. </span><span class="c11">Next, you will need to navigate back to the OpenCV directory and inside the OpenCV directory you will need to create a build directory. This can be achieved by running the commands </span><span class="c45">cd ~/opencv </span><span class="c11">and</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;mkdir build</span><span class="c0">.</span><span class="c11">&nbsp; After creating the build directory, enter it by issuing</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;cd build</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">Once in the build directory, you will want to work in a virtual environment do so by issuing </span><span class="c45">workon cv.</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;Once in the virtual environment, we will use CMake to begin the compilation process. Execute the command shown below. </span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 606.50px; height: 213.83px;"><img alt="" src="images/image48.png" style="width: 606.50px; height: 213.83px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c74 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">When the CMake is finished running, you should see output that looks similar to the output shown below. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.00px; height: 234.67px;"><img alt="" src="images/image46.png" style="width: 624.00px; height: 234.67px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">If the output of the CMake is good, you can proceed to compile OpenCV by running the command </span><span class="c45">make</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c10">The compilation of OpenCV will take a while so don&rsquo;t be alarmed if it does not finish quickly like the previous commands that have been run. After the compilation is complete, you should see an output that looks similar to the output shown below. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.00px; height: 154.67px;"><img alt="" src="images/image65.png" style="width: 624.00px; height: 154.67px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">Finally, you can install OpenCV by running the command </span><span class="c45">sudo make install</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">To verify that OpenCV is properly installed, run the commands</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;import cv2 &nbsp;</span><span class="c11">followed by </span><span class="c45">cv2.__version_.</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">If OpenCV is properly installed, the output of these commands should show the version of OpenCV that is currently installed.</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">Next, we will cover the installation process for TensorFlow. TensorFlow is an open-source library for numerical computation and machine learning. Again, be sure you are working with Python 3.6 before beginning the installation process for TensorFlow as it is not supported by Python 3.7. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">The first step in the installation process for TensorFlow can be done by executing the command </span><span class="c45">pip3 install tensorflow</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c10">Next, you will need to install some of the dependencies needed by TensorFlow. This can be achieved by running the commands shown below.</span></p><p class="c9 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c15"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 607.00px; height: 108.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image24.png" style="width: 607.00px; height: 108.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c9 c13"><span class="c21 c86"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">As mentioned previously, the COCO dataset will be used for object detection. The following commands will help download the COCO API as well as copy it to the appropriate directory for TensorFlow. The to download the COCO API is</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;git clone </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/cocodataset/cocoapi.git&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084648000">https://github.com/cocodataset/cocoapi.git</a></span><span class="c45">. </span><span class="c10">The command</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">cd </span><span class="c45">cocoapi</span><span class="c45">/</span><span class="c45">PythonAPI</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">will allow you to change into the appropriate directory. Once in the correct directory you can run the command </span><span class="c45">make</span><span class="c10">. The last command is </span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">cp -r pycocotools &lt;path to tensorflow&gt;/models/research/ </span><span class="c10">which will copy the files to the research directory.</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">TensorFlow&rsquo;s</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;object detection API uses protocol buffers also called protobufs to configure various parameters. </span><span class="c11">Protbufs</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;are used to serialize data and were designed to be simple and efficient. Before you can use Protobufs, the libraries must first be compiled. To compile the libraries you must change directories into the research directory inside the TensorFlow directory. To navigate to the proper directory, run the following commands</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c45">cd tensorflow cd models cd research</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">Once inside the research directory, run the command </span><span class="c28 c21">protoc object_detection/protos/*.proto --python_out=. </span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">If you receive an error after running the above command, you will need to manually install the protobuf compiler from within the research directory. To do so, first, you will need to download and unzip the protobuf file by executing the commands</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c45">wget -O protobuf.zip </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/google/protobuf/releases/download/v3.0.0/protoc-3.0.0-linux-x86_64.zip&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084649000">https://github.com/google/protobuf/releases/download/v3.0.0/protoc-3.0.0-linux-x86_64.zip</a></span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">and </span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">unzip protobuf.zip.</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;It is important to note that you download the version for the operating system that you are working with. Then you can recompile by re-running the command</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;protoc object_detection/protos/*.proto --python_out=.</span><span class="c0">&nbsp; </span><span class="c11">If you wish to add the libraries to the Python path, you will need to edit the </span><span class="c11">bash profile</span><span class="c11">. As previously explained, open your bash profile and add </span><span class="c45">export PYTHONPATH=$PYTHONPATH:`&lt;complete path to tensorflow/models/research`:`complete path to tensorflow/models/research`/slim</span><span class="c16">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">to your </span><span class="c11">~/.bashrc file. Finally, you can test that you have TensorFlow properly installed by running </span><span class="c45">python3 object_detection/builders/model_builder_test.py</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">If you get any errors for missing dependencies, you can install them by running the command </span><span class="c28 c21">python3 -m pip install name_of_dependency. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Applications for Object Detection on macOS:</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c45">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">Now that you have all the necessary software packages installed, we can use them to implement object detection. &nbsp;You will need to create a directory to store all the relevant files for object detection. You can name the directory &ldquo;yolo_object_detection&rdquo; or something similar. To create this directory simply run the command </span><span class="c45">mkdir yolo_object_detection</span><span class="c11">. Within the directory you just created, create another directory called &ldquo;yolo_coco&rdquo; which will contain the dependencies for the COCO dataset. This directory can be created using the command </span><span class="c45">mkdir yolo_coco. </span><span class="c11">The three things that you will need to download in this folder are </span><span class="c0">coco.names, yolov3.cfg, and </span><span class="c0">yolov3</span><span class="c0">.weights. </span><span class="c11">These files can be downloaded from </span><span class="c11 c98"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/pjreddie/darknet/blob/master/data/coco.names&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084651000">https://github.com/pjreddie/darknet/blob/master/data/coco.names</a></span><span class="c11">, </span><span class="c98 c11"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/pjreddie/darknet/blob/master/cfg/yolov3.cfg&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084651000">https://github.com/pjreddie/darknet/blob/master/cfg/yolov3.cfg</a></span><span class="c11">, and </span><span class="c98 c11"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://pjreddie.com/media/files/yolov3.weights&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084652000">https://pjreddie.com/media/files/yolov3.weights</a></span><span class="c10">&nbsp;respectively. Next, you can create a Python file called &ldquo;yolo_image.py&rdquo; which will contain the code that will be used for object detection. The code used is shown in the figures below. </span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 457.00px; height: 659.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image58.png" style="width: 457.00px; height: 659.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.00px; height: 609.33px;"><img alt="" src="images/image17.png" style="width: 624.00px; height: 609.33px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 562.00px; height: 279.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image71.png" style="width: 562.00px; height: 279.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">To run this code execute the command </span><span class="c28 c21">python3 yolo.py --image images/name_of_image.jpg --yolo yolo-coco.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">A sample output of this code can be seen below. As you can see a box is output around each object that was detected as well as a confidence.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 556.50px; height: 313.09px;"><img alt="" src="images/image62.png" style="width: 556.50px; height: 313.09px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">The YOLO implementation for video is very similar to that of the implementation for images. You will need to create a new Python file called &ldquo;yolo_video.py&rdquo;. The code needed is shown below. </span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 459.00px; height: 685.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image23.png" style="width: 459.00px; height: 685.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 580.00px; height: 711.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image53.png" style="width: 580.00px; height: 711.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.00px; height: 582.67px;"><img alt="" src="images/image37.png" style="width: 624.00px; height: 582.67px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 605.00px; height: 224.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image72.png" style="width: 605.00px; height: 224.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c6 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">To run this code execute the command </span><span class="c28 c21">python3 yolo_video.py --input videos/name_of_video.mp4 \ --output output/output_video.avi --yolo yolo-coco</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">A sample output of the code can be seen below.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c9 c13 c61"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">As you can see, the model is able to track the boat well for the most part. At certain angles and further distances, it loses track of the boat for small periods of time. However, it is able to recover and begin tracking the boat again fairly quickly.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Installation Process for Raspberry Pi:</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c10">This portion of the tutorial will cover the process for implementing object detection using a Raspberry Pi and Raspberry Pi Camera.</span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">The first step is to download the New Out Of the Box Software (NOOBS) zip file which can be found at </span><span class="c98 c11"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.raspberrypi.org/downloads/noobs/&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084655000">https://www.raspberrypi.org/downloads/noobs/</a></span><span class="c10">. NOOBS is an operating system installer which contains the operating system for the Raspberry Pi called Raspbian. You will also need a MicroSD card and a USB adapter for the MicroSD card. I suggest you use a 32 GB MicroSD card at a minimum. You can then use the software package balenaEtcher to flash NOOBS onto the MicroSD card. Once NOOBS has been flashed onto the MicroSD card, you can insert the MicroSD card into the Raspberry Pi. To power the Raspberry Pi, you will need a Micro USB cable. Initially to setup your Raspberry Pi you will need to either connect the Raspberry Pi to a computer that has a mouse and keyboard. If NOOBS was properly flashed onto the MicroSD card, once the Raspberry Pi is connected to a monitor and powered on, the Raspberry Pi will boot and a Raspberry Pi configuration menu should appear. If you have issues, be sure to verify that NOOBS was flashed onto your MicroSD card properly. </span></p><p class="c9 c15"><span class="c11">This next step will cover setting up Secure Shell (SSH) so that you can remotely access your Raspberry Pi using any computer. This step is unnecessary but will make it more convenient for yourself later. So if you want you can skip to the next section. First, you will need to use the preferences menu to navigate to the Raspberry Pi configuration window. Next, select the interfaces tab and ensure that SSH is enabled. After enabling SSH, run the command </span><span class="c45">hostname -I </span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">to get the IP address for your Raspberry Pi. Now you will be able to remotely access your Raspberry Pi by running the command</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c45">ssh pi@ip_address_of_your_pi</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">from the command line of a terminal. When you SSH into your Raspberry Pi, you will be prompted to enter the password which should be</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">set as </span><span class="c45">raspberry </span><span class="c10">by default.</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Now you can begin the process for setting up object detection on your Raspberry Pi. Before beginning, I recommend that you place a heat sink on the processor of the Raspberry Pi as this will be a long process. The heat sink will help prevent the processor from overheating. The first thing to do is to ensure your Raspberry Pi is updated. To do so run the command</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;sudo apt-get update</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">from your Raspberry Pi terminal followed by </span><span class="c45">sudo apt-get dist-upgrade</span><span class="c0">. </span><span class="c11">Next, we will work on installing TensorFlow. Be sure that you are in your /home/pi directory. Within the pi directory you can create a directory called tf to store all the necessary files. To do so run the command </span><span class="c45">mkdir tf </span><span class="c11">and then you can enter that directory using </span><span class="c45">cd tf.</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;Then, you can download a pre-built file for installing TensorFlow on the Raspberry Pi using the command</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c45">wget </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/lhelontra/tensorflow-on-arm/releases/download/v1.10.0/tensorflow-1.10.0-cp35-none-linux_armv7l.whl&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084656000">https://github.com/lhelontra/tensorflow-on-arm/releases/download/v1.10.0/tensorflow-1.10.0-cp35-none-linux_armv7l.whl</a></span><span class="c45">.</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">After downloading the file, you can install it by running the command</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c45">sudo pip3 install /home/pi/tf/tensorflow-1.8.0-cp35-none-linux_armv7l.whl.</span><span class="c0">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">Next, some dependencies needed by TensorFlow will need to be installed. You can install the dependencies by running the following commands shown below.</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 477.00px; height: 62.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image64.png" style="width: 477.00px; height: 62.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">After installing the dependencies, you will have completed everything needed for TensorFlow at this time.</span></p><p class="c26 c13"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c26 c15"><span class="c11">Next, you can move on to the installation for OpenCV. The installation process is very simple. As you did previously for TensorFlow, you will need to install some dependencies that are required by OpenCV. The installation of the dependencies can be completed by executing the following commands</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">which are shown below.</span></p><p class="c15 c26"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 552.50px; height: 69.77px;"><img alt="" src="images/image70.png" style="width: 552.50px; height: 69.77px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c26 c15"><span class="c11">After the dependencies have been installed, simply run the command </span><span class="c45">pip3 install opencv-python. </span><span class="c10">This completes the OpenCV installation.</span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;You will now need to compile and install the protobufs that </span><span class="c11">TensorFlow&rsquo;s</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;object detection is reliant upon. First, you will need to install the packages that are used to compile Protobuf. This can be achieved by running the command </span><span class="c45">sudo apt-get install autoconf automake libtool curl. </span><span class="c11">Then, you will need to download the protobuf using the command </span><span class="c45">wget </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/google/protobuf/releases/download/v3.7.1/protobuf-all-3.7.1.tar.gz&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084657000">https://github.com/google/protobuf/releases/download/v3.7.1/protobuf-all-3.7.1.tar.gz</a></span><span class="c45">. </span><span class="c11">If there is a newer version available, feel free to download it instead. Next, you will need to unpack the file you downloaded using the command </span><span class="c45">tar -zxvf protobuf-all-3.7.1.tar.gz. </span><span class="c11">Then, change directories into the folder by running the command </span><span class="c45">cd protobuf-3.7.1</span><span class="c11">. Once in the protobuf directory, you can configure the build by issuing the command </span><span class="c45">./configure</span><span class="c11">.When the configuration is complete, you can build the package by running the command </span><span class="c45">make</span><span class="c11">. The build process will take a while so don&rsquo;t be alarmed if it runs for about an hour. When the build is finished run the command </span><span class="c45">make check</span><span class="c11">. This process will take even longer than the build. After the make check is completed, you can run the command </span><span class="c45">sudo make install</span><span class="c11">. Next, change directories into the python directory using the command </span><span class="c45">cd python. </span><span class="c11">Once in the python directory, you can export the library path by executing the command </span><span class="c45">export LD_LIBRARY_PATH=../src/.libs.</span><span class="c10">&nbsp;Then, run the commands shown below.</span></p><p class="c87 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 429.00px; height: 65.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image38.png" style="width: 429.00px; height: 65.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c10">Next, run the following commands shown below to set the proper paths.</span></p><p class="c87 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 470.00px; height: 42.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image47.png" style="width: 470.00px; height: 42.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c11">Finally, execute the command </span><span class="c45">sudo ldconfig</span><span class="c11">. To verify that protobuf has been properly installed you can run the command </span><span class="c45">protoc</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;and make sure that there are no errors. In order for TensorFlow to work properly, the Raspberry Pi will need to be rebooted after completing the protobuf installation. To reboot the Raspberry Pi run the command </span><span class="c28 c21">sudo reboot now. </span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c132">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">After the Raspberry Pi reboots, you can set up the TensorFlow directory. From your home directory, create a new directory called tensorflow_test and then enter that directory. This can be done by running the commands </span><span class="c45">mkdir tensorflow_test</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;and </span><span class="c45">cd tensorflow_test. </span><span class="c11">Once in the new</span><span class="c116">&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">directory, you can download the TensorFlow github repository using the command</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;git clone --recurse-submodules </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/tensorflow/models.git&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084659000">https://github.com/tensorflow/models.git</a></span><span class="c45">. </span><span class="c11">Next, you will need to modify PYTHONPATH in the .bashrc file. To open the .bashrc file run the command </span><span class="c45">sudo nano ~/.bashrc. </span><span class="c10">Once in the .bashrc file, navigate to the end of the file and add the line shown below.</span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 624.00px; height: 14.67px;"><img alt="" src="images/image49.png" style="width: 624.00px; height: 14.67px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c10">Then, you can save and exit the bashrc file.</span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c45">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="c11">Next, you will need to use protoc to compile the .proto files needed for object detection. You will need to compile the files in the research directory. To navigate to the research directory, use the command </span><span class="c45">cd /home/pi/tensorflow_test/models/research. </span><span class="c11">Once in the research directory, run the command </span><span class="c45">protoc object_detection/protos/*.proto --python_out=. </span><span class="c11">The previous command converts all the .proto files to .py files (python files). The next steps will need to be done in the object_detection directory. To navigate to the object_detection directory execute the command</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;cd /home/pi/</span><span class="c45">tensorflow1</span><span class="c45">/models/research/object_detection. </span><span class="c11">Once in the object_detection directory, you will need to download one of </span><span class="c11">TensorFlow&rsquo;s</span><span class="c11">&nbsp;pretrained object detection models. Since the Raspberry Pi has a weak processor, you will need to download the fastest model possible. The tradeoff for speed is lower accuracy. However, as stated previously the decrease in accuracy is not a concern. To download the model execute the command </span><span class="c45">wget </span><span class="c38"><a class="c5" href="https://www.google.com/url?q=http://download.tensorflow.org/models/object_detection/ssdlite_mobilenet_v2_coco_2018_05_09.tar.gz&amp;sa=D&amp;ust=1579744084661000">http://download.tensorflow.org/models/object_detection/ssdlite_mobilenet_v2_coco_2018_05_09.tar.gz</a></span><span class="c45">. </span><span class="c11">At the time this is currently the fastest model available, but if a faster model is available in the future you should download it instead. Finally, you can unpack the model by running the command</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;tar -xzvf ssdlite_mobilenet_v2_coco_2018_05_09.tar.gz. </span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Applications on Raspberry Pi:</span></p><p class="c26 c15"><span class="c10">Now that all the necessary packages are installed on the Raspberry Pi we can begin the process of implementing object detection. Within the object_detection directory, you can create a Python file called &ldquo;object_detection.py&rdquo;. The code needed is shown in the figure below. Before running the script, be sure to enable the camera using the Raspberry Pi configuration menu. Also, be sure to connect the Raspberry Pi camera to the Raspberry Pi. </span></p><p class="c87 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 424.42px; height: 617.50px;"><img alt="" src="images/image39.png" style="width: 424.42px; height: 617.50px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c87 c13"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c87 c23 c37"><span class="c21 c74"></span></p><p class="c23 c87"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 578.00px; height: 670.00px;"><img alt="" src="images/image25.png" style="width: 578.00px; height: 670.00px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c11">To run this code execute the command </span><span class="c45">python3 object_detection.py.</span></p><p class="c26 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c26 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c26 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c26 c23"><span class="c10">A sample output can be seen below.</span></p><p class="c87 c23"><span style="overflow: hidden; display: inline-block; margin: 0.00px 0.00px; border: 0.00px solid #000000; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); width: 518.50px; height: 293.22px;"><img alt="" src="images/image55.png" style="width: 518.50px; height: 293.22px; margin-left: 0.00px; margin-top: 0.00px; transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px); -webkit-transform: rotate(0.00rad) translateZ(0px);" title=""></span></p><p class="c9 c23 c37"><span class="c14"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c28 c21"></span></p><p class="c34 c23"><span class="c45">Appendix E:</span><span class="c45">&nbsp;SolidWorks Drawings [SO]</span></p><ol class="c33 lst-kix_2eoz2it30pp1-0 start" start="1"><li class="c34 c12"><span class="c45">Zoomed in Servo Baseboard SolidWorks drawing</span><span style="overflow: hidden; This timeline is helpful in the program management side of things and will be beneficial for future years of UHDT (though edited specifically for UHDT-M). Much of the program management logistics will be accomplished in the beginning of each semester. It is important to maintain team morale with successful team bondings and flight tests throughout the semester. The spring semester timeline has significantly less events and things to do, due to the importance of the final push for flight tests. UHDT-M has had 2-3 flight tests per week for every week possible. </span></p><p class="c34 c23 c37"><span class="c10"></span></p><p class="c6 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Fall Semester Timeline</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 1</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c61"><span class="c10">Establishment of meeting times, welcome meeting, determination of members and introduction to subsystem leaders, project manager and chief engineer.</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_32rvpwx58ejx-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Explain the design notebook - list important notes from meetings and all reasoning/rationale for decisions made for mission ops</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c11">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="c0 c21">Roles to be assigned at beginning of semester/year:</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_yfn36ojc19ys-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c0 c21">Scheduler (flight tests, team meetings/outings, rehearsals, cleaners; use of when2meet)</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c0 c21">Safety Pilots</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c0 c21">Webmaster</span></li></ul><ul class="c33 lst-kix_yfn36ojc19ys-1 start"><li class="c9 c81 c23"><span class="c0 c21">Will probably need a new team picture for the website</span></li></ul><ul class="c33 lst-kix_yfn36ojc19ys-0"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c0 c21">VIP Presentation Board (for &ldquo;presentation&rdquo; fair in November)</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c0 c21">Recorder (to take minutes at meetings, notes during rehearsals and presentations, etc)</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c0 c21">Videographer</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c0">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="c10">First Admin/Leadership Meeting</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_2egepujlxt8b-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Introduction of members, advisors, mentors, etc</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Establish mission and functional requirements, beginning with problem statement</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 2</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c61"><span class="c10">First subsystem meetings, establish expectations from members and set initial tasks, explain the purpose of the mission operations lead.</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_j1oqutjgnuee-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Create the mission plan</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Checklists</span></li><li class="c9 c19"><span class="c10">Flight tests and safety pilot logs</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Explanation of overall system -&gt; give members context</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 3</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c61"><span class="c10">Explanation of and delegation of tasks for the project proposal presentation and report</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 4</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c61"><span class="c10">Ensure understanding of mission and mission requirements</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 5</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Proposal presentation, final edits for the report</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_9k972mmy65vl-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c11 c32">9/18: Proposal Presentation</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c32 c11">9/22: Proposal Report due</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c32 c11">9/22: SPRG Proposal</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 6</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c61"><span class="c10">Review previous years&rsquo; t-log files and read through mission planner manual, create first draft of mission plan</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_6f810vi3dt7n-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Explain what a t-log file is and how it relates to our mission plan</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 7</span></p><p class="c9 c23 c61"><span class="c10">Check changes in the competition rules, determine all necessary items for each task being attempted and required time, meet with subsystem leads and begin research for PDR. </span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_vjiu27uyuxzp-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c11">PDR: what changes need to be made from last year&rsquo;s UAS, research and rationale for each adjustment</span></li></ul><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c28 c21">Week 8</span></p><p class="c9 c23"><span class="c10">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;PDR research and rationale, slides and drafting MO portion of the PDR</span></p><ul class="c33 lst-kix_7yxd5xt2joke-0 start"><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Should have rationale for our mission requirements (e.g. why we chose to have a PDB as opposed to other options for electronics, why the landing gear is being attempted and how it can help us)</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c10">Include reasoning for direction of flight, order of tasks, altitude, search area task route, changes to air delivery, changes to mission plan</span></li><li class="c19 c9"><span class="c11 c125">10/10: UROP Proposal (if
